# Celeste's Girl




## Introduction

I wrote *Celesteâ€™s Girl* because Iâ€™m tired of two lies: that womanhood is a costume, and that trans-women are a threat rather than an ally.

I know, intimately, what male privilege feels like from the insideâ€”and what it means to step away from it. No one abandons that invisible armour for â€œskirts and perfume.â€ They do it for survival, for coherence, for the simple, radical relief of finally being able to recognise themselves in the mirror and in their own life.

This book isnâ€™t interested in spectacle. Itâ€™s interested in infrastructure: in the women who quietly build worlds where other women can breathe. Wardrobe is a deliberate answer to the patriarchyâ€™s workshops and back rooms. Itâ€™s a place where competence is normal, where care is policy, and where a trans girl isnâ€™t a punchline or a fetish object or a theoryâ€”sheâ€™s a colleague, a daughter, a wife, one of the women.

I wanted to show what happens when a former â€œbeneficiaryâ€ of patriarchy walks into a women-led space and says, without quite knowing it yet, *I belong with you*. What does it cost her? What does it cost them? What do they gain together that none of them could have had alone?

Celesteâ€™s Girl is my love letter to womenâ€™s work, to chosen family, and to the fierce, unglamorous kind of solidarity that stands in front of loading bays and board tables and says: â€œNo. Not here. Not to her.â€ Itâ€™s also a quiet insistence that trans women are not an enemy camp, but some of the keenest, most motivated allies women will ever have in dismantling the structures that harm us all.
